reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Scalar-gated delta-rule linear attention recurrence.

    A recurrent state matrix is decayed by a scalar gate per head, updated by a
    rank-1 key/value outer product, then read out by a query vector.
    """

    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()

    def forward(
        self,
        q: torch.Tensor,
        k: torch.Tensor,
        v: torch.Tensor,
        gate: torch.Tensor,
        beta: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim = q.shape
        state = torch.zeros(batch_size, num_heads, head_dim, head_dim, dtype=q.dtype, device=q.device)
        outputs = []

        for t in range(seq_len):
            gate_t = gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-1).unsqueeze(-1)
            beta_t = beta[:, t].unsqueeze(-1).unsqueeze(-1)
            kv_outer = k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2)
            state = gate_t * state + beta_t * kv_outer
            y_t = (q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2)
            outputs.append(y_t)

        return torch.stack(outputs, dim=1)


batch_size = 2
seq_len = 128
num_heads = 4
head_dim = 24


def get_inputs():
    q = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    k = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    v = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    gate = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, dtype=torch.float32))
    beta = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, dtype=torch.float32))
    return [q, k, v, gate, beta]


def get_init_inputs():
    return []

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for the reference PyTorch model. The model is a simple scalar-gated delta-rule linear attention recurrence. The forward pass iterates over time steps, updating a state matrix and computing an output per time step. We need to write a `ModelNew` class, with the same constructor (no args needed), and a forward method that calls a custom CUDA kernel. We need to use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` to compile and bind CUDA source at module load time. The kernel should be faster than the reference PyTorch implementation, which is a sequential Python loop over seq_len with small tensor ops. This is very slow because it launches many small CUDA kernels and does a lot of memory traffic. A fused CUDA kernel that processes the sequence in parallel (or at least fuses the operations per time step) should be much faster. Given the recurrence: state_t = gate_t * state_{t-1} + beta_t * (k_t outer v_t) y_t = sum_i q_t[i] * state_t[i, j]? Wait. Let's define shapes: q, k, v: (B, T, H, D) gate, beta: (B, T, H) state: (B, H, D, D) At time t: gate_t: (B, H, 1, 1) beta_t: (B, H, 1, 1) k_t: (B, H, D, 1) (unsqueeze(-1)) v_t: (B, H, 1, D) (unsqueeze(-2)) kv_outer = k_t * v_t -> (B, H, D, D) state = gate_t * state + beta_t * kv_outer q_t: (B, H, D, 1) (unsqueeze(-1)) y_t = (q_t * state).sum(dim=-2) -> q_t has shape (B, H, D, 1), state has shape (B, H, D, D). Multiplying elementwise: (B, H, D, D). Sum over dim=-2, which is the first D (rows). So y_t shape is (B, H, D). Then stack over time -> (B, T, H, D). So state_t = gate_t * state_{t-1} + beta_t * k_t v_t^T. And y_t = q_t^T state_t. This is a linear RNN / linear attention. We need to implement this in CUDA. We can process each head and batch element independently. For each (b, h), we have a sequence of length T, with vectors of dimension D. The recurrence on the state matrix S_t (D x D) is: S_t = g_t * S_{t-1} + b_t * k_t v_t^T with S_{-1} = 0. Then y_t = q_t^T S_t. We can unroll: S_t = \sum_{s=0}^t ( \prod_{u=s+1}^t g_u ) * b_s * k_s v_s^T Then y_t = q_t^T S_t = \sum_{s=0}^t ( \prod_{u=s+1}^t g_u ) * b_s * (q_t^T k_s) v_s^T Wait, q_t^T k_s is a scalar (dot product). Then y_t = \sum_{s=0}^t ( ... ) * (q_t^T k_s) * v_s. So y_t is a vector in R^D. This means we could compute y_t by accumulating over s <= t without explicitly storing the full D x D state, if we compute the scalar products on the fly. Specifically: y_t = \sum_{s=0}^t \alpha_{t,s} * (q_t \cdot k_s) * v_s where \alpha_{t,s} = b_s * \prod_{u=s+1}^t g_u. This is similar to an attention mechanism with a decay factor. But computing this directly for each t would be O(T^2 * D) per (b,h), which for T=128, D=24 is 128^2 * 24 = 393k operations, while the D x D matrix recurrence is O(T * D^2) = 128 * 24^2 = 73k operations. So the matrix recurrence is actually cheaper if D < T. Here D=24, T=128, D^2=576 < T*D=3072? Actually T * D^2 = 128 * 576 = 73728. T^2 * D = 16384 * 24 = 393216. So the matrix approach is about 5x cheaper in compute. However, the matrix approach uses D^2 = 576 memory per head, and the naive Python implementation does many small operations. Let's examine: For each (b,h,i,j), we have a sequence a_t = beta_t * k_t[i] * v_t[j]. And we want to compute the gated prefix sum: S_t = g_t * S_{t-1} + a_t, with S_{-1}=0. This is a linear recurrence of the form S_t = c_t + g_t * S_{t-1}, where c_t = a_t. This is not a simple prefix sum, but a scan with a multiplicative operator. Specifically, it's a scan over the monoid where each element is a pair (g_t, c_t) and the combination is: (g2, c2) ∘ (g1, c1) = (g2 * g1, g2 * c1 + c2) Then the scan gives the cumulative effect. But we can compute it as: S_t = \sum_{s=0}^t ( \prod_{u=s+1}^t g_u ) * c_s. To compute all S_t in parallel, we can use a parallel scan where each thread holds a pair (g, c) and the associative operator is as above. Is this operator associative? Let's check: (g3, c3) ∘ ( (g2, c2) ∘ (g1, c1) ) = ? (g2, c2) ∘ (g1, c1) = (g2*g1, g2*c1 + c2) Then (g3, c3) ∘ (g2*g1, g2*c1 + c2) = (g3*g2*g1, g3*(g2*c1 + c2) + c3) = (g3*g2*g1, g3*g2*c1 + g3*c2 + c3). Now ( (g3, c3) ∘ (g2, c2) ) ∘ (g1, c1) = ? (g3, c3) ∘ (g2, c2) = (g3*g2, g3*c2 + c3) Then (g3*g2, g3*c2 + c3) ∘ (g1, c1) = (g3*g2*g1, g3*g2*c1 + g3*c2 + c3). Same. So yes, associative. Thus we can do a parallel inclusive scan on the sequence of pairs (g_t, c_t) with the operator: (g_a, c_a) ⊗ (g_b, c_b) = (g_a * g_b, g_a * c_b + c_a) Wait, we need to be careful about order. Let each segment represent the transformation from a state before the segment to after the segment. A single step t: state' = g_t * state + c_t. This can be represented as an affine transformation (g_t, c_t), where applying it to state x gives g_t * x + c_t. Composition: apply transform A then transform B: B(A(x)) = g_B * (g_A * x + c_A) + c_B = (g_B * g_A) * x + (g_B * c_A + c_B). So the composition of A then B is (g_B * g_A, g_B * c_A + c_B). If we want to compute the total transformation from the start to each point, we need a prefix composition of the steps. For inclusive scan, the result at position t should be the composition of steps 0..t. So the associative operator for a scanned pair from left to right should be: combine(left, right) = (right.g * left.g, right.g * left.c + right.c) Let's define a tuple T = (g, c). T_left is earlier, T_right is later. The combined transformation for applying T_left then T_right is: T_comb.g = T_right.g * T_left.g T_comb.c = T_right.g * T_left.c + T_right.c If we do a prefix scan with this operator, the result at t will be the composition of [0..t], which applied to initial state 0 gives: S_t = scan_g * 0 + scan_c = scan_c. So we only need the c component of the scan result. Thus we can compute S_t for all t with a parallel scan on c_t with the combine op. But note: c_t = beta_t * k_t[i] * v_t[j]. This depends on i and j. The g_t = gate_t depends only on (b,h,t). So per (b,h,i,j), we have a sequence of c_t where c_t shares v_t[j] and k_t[i] across time. If we launch blocks for each (b,h,i,j), each block has T=128 threads. Each thread t loads: - gate[t] (shared across i,j in the same (b,h), so cached via L2) - beta[t] (shared) - k[t][i] (depends on i) - v[t][j] (depends on j) And computes c_t = beta[t] * k[t][i] * v[t][j]. Then performs a block-level scan of 128 elements with the combine op using g_t. Then S_t = scan_c (the c component after scan). Then we need y_t[j] = sum_i q_t[i] * S_t[i][j]. Wait, S_t is per (i,j). After the scan, each thread t (representing time t) holds S_t for its (b,h,i,j). But we need to sum over i to get y_t[j] for each t. That means we need a reduction across i for each (b,h,t,j). But our blocks are organized by (b,h,i,j), not by time. So after computing S_t, each block has 128 values S_t for t=0..127. We need to reduce across i dimension for each t. This is a transpose-like reduction. Alternatively, we could first compute the scan per (b,h,j) using a block that handles all i, as before. Reference implementation: For each t (128): - gate_t = gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-1).unsqueeze(-1) -> (B,H,1,1) - beta_t = beta[:, t].unsqueeze(-1).unsqueeze(-1) -> (B,H,1,1) - kv_outer = k[:,t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:,t].unsqueeze(-2) -> (B,H,D,D) - state = gate_t * state + beta_t * kv_outer -> (B,H,D,D) - y_t = (q[:,t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2) -> (B,H,D) This is 128 steps, each doing a few PyTorch ops. We need to pass `grid` and `block` and `shared_mem`. Let's see. In PyTorch `load_inline`, we define a host wrapper function that takes the tensors and calls the kernel. We need to compute `shared_mem` there. Alternatively, we can have a fixed maximum size in the kernel, e.g., `__shared__ float s_val[128];` and document that D must be <= 128. Since typical head_dim is small (<=128), this is fine and avoids dynamic shared memory. Let's do that. So kernel code: ```cpp template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ q, const scalar_t* __restrict__ k, const scalar_t* __restrict__ v, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, scalar_t* __restrict__ out, int B, int T, int H, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int i = threadIdx.x; if (i >= D) return; int stride_t = H * D; int base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D; int stride_gate = H; int base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; int base_out = base_qkv; float S = 0.0f; __shared__ float s_val[128]; // assume D <= 128 for (int t = 0; t < T; ++t) { int offset = t * stride_t; int offset_gate = t * stride_gate; float g = (float)gate[base_gate + offset_gate]; float b_t = (float)beta[base_gate + offset_gate]; float k_i = (float)k[base_qkv + offset + i]; float v_j = (float)v[base_qkv + offset + j]; float q_i = (float)q[base_qkv + offset + i]; S = g * S + b_t * k_i * v_j; s_val[i] = S * q_i; __syncthreads(); if (i == 0) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ii = 0; ii < D; ++ii) { sum += s_val[ii]; } out[base_out + offset + j] = (scalar_t)sum; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, note that `threadIdx.x` can be >= D if blockDim.x > D. If we set blockDim.x = D, then `i >= D` never happens. But we want to use `blockDim.x = D`. In the host wrapper, we can call `scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(...)` where `block = (D,)` if D is known. D comes from `q.size(-1)`. So we can set block = (D,). With D=24, block=(24,). But if D is not a multiple of 32, `__syncthreads()` works fine. However, one issue: In the loop, we have `__syncthreads()` after writing to shared mem, and before reading. Thread 0 does the sum and writes to global mem. Then another `__syncthreads()` before the next iteration. This is correct. But there is divergence: only thread 0 does the sum loop. However, the loop is small (D <= 128). The `__syncthreads()` ensures all threads wait. Divergence is minimal because the difference is just a small serial loop in one thread. Potential performance issue: global memory writes in the loop. `out[base_out + offset + j]` is written by thread 0 only. The address is unique per block. That's fine. Memory coalescing: `out` write is a single scalar per block per t. Now, what about `q`, `k`, `v` reads. Threads i=0..D-1 load `q[base + offset + i]`, which are consecutive floats. This is coalesced. Same for `k`. `v[base + offset + j]` is broadcast (all threads load same address). This is also fine (cached). `gate` and `beta` reads are also broadcast. This kernel should be very fast. But we have only 192 blocks. Is there a way to further parallelize? We can unroll the loop over t by a factor to reduce loop overhead. `#pragma unroll` with a fixed T? T is dynamic. The total number of threads is fixed: B*H*D*D = 4608. We cannot increase total parallelism beyond that because each state element S[i][j] requires a sequential recurrence. The only extra parallelism is over time (T=128). So the theoretical max independent thread count is B*H*D*D*T = 589,824 if we parallelize over time too. But that requires a parallel scan. Let's investigate whether a parallel scan over time is worth it. We have per (b,h,i,j) a scalar recurrence: S_t = g_t S_{t-1} + c_t, S_{-1}=0. We want to compute y_t[j] = sum_i q_t[i] S_t[i][j]. But if we compute S_t for all t in parallel per (i,j) using a scan, then we need to combine with q_t and reduce over i. That reduction could be done per t after the scan. For each t, we need to reduce over i. If we have one block per (b,h,t,j), handling all i, then each block size = D threads. That gives grid = B*H*T*D = 2*4*128*24 = 24,576 blocks! That's huge parallelism. Total threads = 24,576 * 24 = 589,824. That would saturate the GPU. But can a block compute S_t[i][j] for all i in parallel without sequential dependence? Let's see: S_t[i][j] depends on S_{t-1}[i][j]. So for a fixed (b,h,t,j), we need S_t for all i. But S_t[i][j] = f(g_{0..t}, c_{0..t}[i][j]). We can compute S_t[i][j] directly as the prefix sum: S_t[i][j] = sum_{s=0}^t (prod_{u=s+1}^t g_u) * c_s[i][j] where c_s[i][j] = beta_s * k_s[i] * v_s[j]. To compute this without knowing S_{t-1}, we need the gate products and prefix sums. For a fixed t, we could precompute the prefix-sum weights W_{t,s} = beta_s * prod_{u=s+1}^t g_u. Then S_t[i][j] = sum_{s=0}^t W_{t,s} * k_s[i] * v_s[j]. This is a weighted sum over s of outer products. For fixed t and j, y_t[j] = sum_i q_t[i] * S_t[i][j] = sum_i q_t[i] * sum_{s=0}^t W_{t,s} * k_s[i] * v_s[j] = sum_{s=0}^t W_{t,s} * v_s[j] * (sum_i q_t[i] * k_s[i]). So if we precompute QK similarities m_{t,s} = dot(q_t, k_s), then y_t[j] = sum_{s=0}^t W_{t,s} * v_s[j] * m_{t,s}. This is a scalar recurrence per (t,j) if we process t sequentially: Let acc_j(t) = something. Wait, from y_t[j] = sum_i q_t[i] * S_t[i][j]. If we have S_t[i][j], we need q_t[i]. So the reduction over i must happen per t. This is about 3-4x more compute than the sequential approach. So not better. Given the total FLOPs is tiny (~3M), the bottleneck is memory latency and kernel launch overhead. The fused sequential kernel with one launch is the best approach. Now, can we further optimize the sequential kernel? The compiler ensures that the intrinsic is only called by active threads. The inactive threads are not executing the instruction. However, the `__shfl_xor_sync` intrinsic requires a `mask` parameter that specifies which threads are participating. The return value from inactive threads is undefined if the source lane is inactive. If we use `0xFFFFFFFF`, it includes all 32 lanes. The source lanes for offset=1 are adjacent lanes. For lane 23, source is lane 22 and 24. Lane 24 is inactive. According to CUDA docs: "If the target thread is inactive, the returned value is undefined." So for lane 23, reading from lane 24 is undefined. That's a problem. Thus shuffle reduction with blockDim.x not a power of two is tricky. To fix this, we can set blockDim.x = 32, and have the first D threads do work; the rest set val = 0. Then we can safely shuffle across all 32 lanes, because all 32 lanes are active. Active threads are 0..31? Wait, if blockDim.x=32, all 32 threads are active. We just need to ensure threads D..31 have val=0 and don't write to output. Let's do that: `block = (32,)`. Wait, but each thread i has its own S register. For i>=D, we don't have a valid column element. We can just keep S=0, and not compute anything. So kernel with block=32: ```cpp template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ q, const scalar_t* __restrict__ k, const scalar_t* __restrict__ v, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, scalar_t* __restrict__ out, int B, int T, int H, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int i = threadIdx.x; int stride_t = H * D; int base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D; int stride_gate = H; int base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; int base_out = base_qkv; float S = 0.0f; bool valid = (i < D); for (int t = 0; t < T; ++t) { int offset = t * stride_t; int offset_gate = t * stride_gate; float g = (float)gate[base_gate + offset_gate]; float b_t = (float)beta[base_gate + offset_gate]; float v_j = (float)v[base_qkv + offset + j]; float k_i = 0.0f; float q_i = 0.0f; if (valid) { k_i = (float)k[base_qkv + offset + i]; q_i = (float)q[base_qkv + offset + i]; S = g * S + b_t * k_i * v_j; } float val = valid ? S * q_i : 0.0f; // warp shuffle reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset_shfl = 16; offset_shfl > 0; offset_shfl >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset_shfl); } if (i == 0) { out[base_out + offset + j] = (scalar_t)val; } } } ``` Wait, `__shfl_down_sync` with `0xFFFFFFFF` and block size 32 means all 32 threads participate. For offset=16: lane i reads from lane i+16. Both are within 0..31, active. Good. For offset=8: lane i reads from lane i+8. Good. For offset=4,2,1. All valid. Since threads i>=D have val=0, they contribute zero, and the sum is correct. This avoids shared memory entirely! And `__syncthreads()` is not needed because the block is one warp. Actually wait: block size is 32, which is exactly one warp. So no `__syncthreads` is needed at all. The warp executes in lockstep (no divergence since the `if (valid)` is just a predicate). No, divergent loads within a warp are handled by masking. The memory unit will service the active lanes. Since the active lanes are contiguous (0..23), the load is coalesced. Similarly for q. So we should make the kernel generic for any D. To support generic D, we can use shared memory reduction. Let's design a kernel that works for any D. Generic kernel with shared memory: Grid: (B, H, D) Block: (block_size,), where block_size is e.g. 32 or a multiple. We can set block size to the smallest power of two >= D, up to 128. Or simply block_size = D (but if D is not a power of two, syncthreads reduction is tricky). Alternatively, we can have block_size = D, but reduction is done by serial sum from thread 0, as described. This avoids power-of-two issues. And it works for any D (as long as D <= max threads per block, say 1024). Since D is typically small (<=128), this is fine. So block size = D. Grid = (B, H, D). Block count = B*H*D. Inside kernel: ```cpp __shared__ float s_val[1024]; // allocate max possible ... s_val[i] = val; __syncthreads(); if (i == 0) { float sum = 0; for (int ii = 0; ii < D; ++ii) sum += s_val[ii]; out[...] = sum; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is simple, safe, and generic. The performance hit of serial reduction by thread 0 is negligible for small D. But if D=24, block size=24. Since 24 < 32, each block is a partial warp. `__syncthreads()` inside a partial warp is supported. It translates to `__bar.sync` with the number of threads. It's fine. However, there is a more subtle issue: shared memory bank conflicts. D=24, all threads write to s_val[i] where i is sequential, so no bank conflicts (assuming 32 banks and float size 4 bytes). Good. Let's write the kernel using this design. We can define it as a template to work with float (since inputs are fp32). But to be safe with different dtypes, we can template it. However, the problem says "produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision". The inputs are float32. So we can just write for float. Let's write the host wrapper: ```cpp void scalar_gated_delta_attention( torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v, torch::Tensor gate, torch::Tensor beta, torch::Tensor out) { int B = q.size(0); int T = q.size(1); int H = q.size(2); int D = q.size(3); dim3 grid(B, H, D); dim3 block(D); scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), gate.data_ptr<float>(), beta.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, T, H, D); } ``` Note: `dim3 block(D)` will fail to compile if `D` is not a `unsigned int`? `dim3` constructor takes `unsigned int`. `D` is `int`, but implicit conversion is fine. But in PyTorch C++ extension, we need to include `<torch/extension.h>`. The kernel should be defined in `cuda_sources` and the host wrapper in `cpp_sources`. Let's assemble the code. One more thing: We need to handle the case where `gate` and `beta` are not contiguous in the last dimension? Actually gate shape is (B, T, H). They are likely contiguous. We can assume they are contiguous or use `.contiguous()` in Python. In `ModelNew.forward`, we should ensure tensors are contiguous and float32. But the reference inputs are float32. We can do: ```python q = q.contiguous() k = k.contiguous() v = v.contiguous() gate = gate.contiguous() beta = beta.contiguous() out = torch.empty_like(q) cuda_module.scalar_gated_delta_attention(q, k, v, gate, beta, out) return out ``` Let's also consider if the output is correctly shaped: (B, T, H, D). `out` should be same shape as q. Now, will this be faster than reference? Yes, because it fuses everything into one kernel. But is there any risk that it's still slower because of low occupancy? The reference is extremely slow (Python loop of 128 steps with multiple PyTorch ops per step). A single kernel launch with any reasonable occupancy will be much faster. Let's estimate: reference launches ~640 kernels. Each kernel launch overhead is maybe 5-10 microseconds. 640 * 5us = 3.2ms. Plus actual compute. Our kernel launch is 1 launch. Compute time is tiny. So speedup could be >100x. That would be great. But let's think: can we further optimize the kernel to ensure we don't have any unnecessary overhead? One issue: `dim3 block(D)` with D=24 leads to partial warp occupancy. The warp scheduler still schedules the warp, but only 24 lanes have active threads. However, `__syncthreads()` might be a bottleneck because it requires all threads in the block to reach it. Since the block is partial warp, there is no inter-warp sync, but the hardware still issues a `bar.sync`. This might be cheap. Another issue: The kernel writes to `out` inside the loop. Global memory writes are not coalesced across threads in a block? In our mapping, each block writes to a unique address `out[base_out + offset + j]`. Since j is fixed per block, each block writes a single float at a fixed address. No coalescing issues because it's just one write per block. But there are many blocks (192), each writing at different addresses. This is fine. Memory reads: For each t, threads in a block read q, k at consecutive addresses (coalesced). v is same address for all threads (broadcast). gate and beta are same address (broadcast). So memory is efficient. Another optimization: We can preload gate and beta into shared memory or registers? Each block reads gate and beta for each t. Since there are D blocks per (b,h), each reads the same gate and beta values. This is redundant. However, with B*H=8 and T=128, total gate reads = 192 blocks * 128 = 24,576 reads of gate values. Each gate value is 4 bytes. Total gate traffic = 96 KB. Very small. Caching will handle this. So not a concern. Alternatively, we can have each block load gate and beta into shared memory for the entire sequence, but 128 floats is 512 bytes, small. But it would require loading them once per block instead of per step. However, we still need them per step. If we preload into shared mem, we save global memory loads if the cache would miss. The kernel computes per-element of the state matrix S[i][j] separately across threads. But let's double check the math. In the reference: - gate_t: [B, 1, 1] per head - beta_t: [B, 1, 1] per head - kv_outer: [B, H, D, D] (k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2)) - state = gate_t * state + beta_t * kv_outer - y_t = (q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2) Here q[:, t] is [B, H, D] and .unsqueeze(-1) makes it [B, H, D, 1]. state is [B, H, D, D]. q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state multiplies [B, H, D, 1] by [B, H, D, D], broadcasting over the last dimension. This gives [B, H, D, D]. Then sum(dim=-2) sums over dimension -2 (index 2, the second to last), which is of size D. So y_t is [B, H, D]. Wait, sum(dim=-2) sums over the D dimension that's being multiplied by q. Let's check: q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) has shape [B, H, D, 1] state has shape [B, H, D, D] The broadcasting: q is replicated over the last dim. The element-wise product has shape [B, H, D, D]. sum(dim=-2) means sum over index 2 (0-indexed), which has size D. So the result is [B, H, D]. This is equivalent to: for each j, sum over i of (q_{t,i} * state_{i,j}). In my kernel, I have threads i, and for each j (blockIdx.z), thread i computes state[i][j], multiplies by q[i], stores in shared memory, and thread 0 sums over i. This should be correct. In a SIMT architecture, all threads in a warp execute the same instruction. If only thread 0 is doing the loop, threads 1-31 are still executing the loop instructions but with predicate masking. So 1 warp reading 1 float still reads 128 bytes! That's a huge waste of memory bandwidth. In the 32-thread block, all 32 threads read simultaneously, fully utilizing the memory transaction. So 6 blocks of 32 threads each would have much better memory bandwidth utilization than 192 blocks of 1 thread each. Let me think about this more carefully: - In the 32-thread block, for coalesced reads of q and k, all 32 lanes read contiguous floats. This results in 1-2 memory transactions per warp. - In the 1-thread block, thread 0 reads 1 float, but the warp memory system still fetches a full cache line (128 bytes). So 31/32 of the fetched data is wasted. So we definitely want to coalesce memory accesses. This means having 32 threads read adjacent memory locations simultaneously. For our problem: - We have 192 work units: (b, h, j) - We can group them into 6 blocks of 32 threads - Or 12 blocks of 16 threads - Or we could have each warp handle a specific (b, h) and process 32 different j values... but we only have 24 j values. Wait, here's an idea: within a warp, have 24 threads each handle one j, but all for the SAME (b, h). Then the remaining 8 threads are idle. But the memory reads of q and k would be coalesced (threads 0-23 read q[0..23] and k[0..23]). And v reads would be scattered (thread j reads v[j]). Gate and beta are uniform. But with this organization: - A block of 32 threads (1 warp) handles one (b, h, all j) - Each thread j computes for j = threadIdx.x - If threadIdx.x >= D, it's masked - Threads loop over T together Memory access per timestep: - q_t: 32 threads read q[0..31] (coalesced, 128 bytes fetched but only 96 bytes used) - k_t: same, coalesced - v_t: 32 threads read v[0..31] (coalesced) - gate_t: all threads read the same address (broadcast, cached) - beta_t: same, broadcast - Output: each thread writes out[j] (scattered/coalesced depending on layout) Actually, if we store output as [B, T, H, D], then for fixed (b, h, t), the j dimension is the contiguous last dimension. So thread j writing out[j] is coalesced! Great. But wait, all 32 threads are writing at the same time t. If they loop over t in lockstep, then at each t they all write to the same t but different j. Since j is contiguous, this is coalesced. However, we need to be careful about the state. Each thread j needs to maintain state[i, j] for all i. That's D floats per thread. For D=24, that's fine in registers. But what about the reduction? In this new organization, each thread j computes ALL i values for itself, so it doesn't need any reduction! It maintains state[0..D-1] in registers and computes dot(q_t, state) directly. No sync needed! So a block of 32 threads: - Thread j (0 <= j < D) handles (b, h, j) - Each thread has its own state[24] in registers - Loop over T: - Load q_t[i] for all i (or load one at a time) - Load v_t[j] (one float) - Load gate_t, beta_t (one float each) - Update state[i] = gate * state[i] + beta * k_t[i] * v_t[j] for all i - Compute out = sum_i q_t[i] * state[i] - Write out_t[j] This is exactly the "one thread per (b, h, j)" approach! If we map thread n in the warp to the n-th (b, h, j) unit in some global ordering, then at each timestep: - Thread n reads q[t, b_n, h_n, i] for all i - But different n have different (b_n, h_n), so reads are NOT coalesced To get coalesced reads, adjacent threads should access adjacent memory. The input layout is [B, T, H, D]. For fixed (b, h, t), the D dimension is contiguous. So if adjacent threads are reading the same (b, h, t) but different i or j, the reads are coalesced. For q and k: [B, T, H, D], so q[b, t, h, i] and q[b, t, h, i+1] are adjacent. If adjacent threads read different i for the same (b, h, t), that's coalesced. For v: [B, T, H, D], so v[b, t, h, j] and v[b, t, h, j+1] are adjacent. If adjacent threads read different j for the same (b, h, t), that's coalesced. At 1 op/cycle and 1.5 GHz, that's ~4 microseconds per warp. With 8 warps on 8 SMs, total time is ~4 microseconds. But that assumes perfect bandwidth utilization. With only 1 warp per SM, we can't saturate memory bandwidth. Modern GPUs need many concurrent warps to hide memory latency and saturate bandwidth. With only 1 warp per SM, memory requests are serialized within the warp, and there are no other warps to fill the pipeline while waiting for memory. So memory latency might dominate. If a memory load takes 200 cycles, and we have no other warps to run, the warp stalls for 200 cycles. If the compute is only 100 cycles, we spend 2/3 of the time stalling. This is why having more warps per SM helps. With 8 warps per SM (64 active threads, which is still only 2 warps if each has 32 threads... wait, 8 warps = 256 threads), we could hide latency much better. But we only have 8 warps total! So even if we put all 8 warps on one SM, the other 131 SMs are idle, but at least that one SM has 8 warps for latency hiding. With 8 warps on 8 SMs, each SM has 1 warp and terrible latency hiding. This is a classic problem with tiny batch sizes. The best we can do is either: 1. Spread warps thinly across all SMs to maximize parallelism but poor latency hiding 2. Concentrate warps on fewer SMs for better latency hiding but leave other SMs idle For bandwidth-bound kernels, concentration is often better because memory bandwidth is a per-GPU resource, not per-SM. All SMs share the same memory bus. So having 8 SMs each issue memory requests vs 1 SM issuing 8x the requests doesn't change total bandwidth. Wait, actually it does matter. If each SM can issue a certain number of memory requests per cycle, and we have 8 SMs active, we get 8x the request rate compared to 1 SM. So spreading across SMs can actually help saturate bandwidth! But with only 1 warp per SM, the warp might not be able to issue requests fast enough to saturate the SM's memory interface. On Hopper, each SM has L1 cache and shared memory bandwidth of ~10+ TB/s. A single warp can do at most 32 loads per instruction, which at 1.5 GHz is 48e9 loads/second. At 4 bytes each, that's 192 GB/s. An SM can handle much more than that. So 1 warp per SM is indeed memory-interface limited. To saturate an SM's memory bandwidth, we need multiple warps so that when one warp stalls on memory, another can issue requests. OK so this is complicated. Wait, one more optimization: I can preload q, k, v, gate, beta into shared memory for the block, and then have threads read from shared memory. For the block (b,h): - q and k: T * D floats each = 128 * 24 * 4 = 12.3 KB - v: T * D floats = 12.3 KB - gate, beta: T floats each = 512 bytes each - Total: ~25.5 KB per block - Shared memory per SM: 228 KB - So we can fit ~8 blocks per SM in shared memory - But we only have 8 blocks total Loading all of q, k, v, gate, beta into shared memory at the start of the block would require one big coalesced read per array, and then all subsequent accesses are from fast shared memory. This could be a huge win! The kernel would be: 1. Load q[b, h, 0..T-1, 0..D-1] into shared memory (coalesced) 2. Load k[b, h, 0..T-1, 0..D-1] into shared memory 3. Load v[b, h, 0..T-1, 0..D-1] into shared memory 4. Load gate[b, h, 0..T-1] into shared memory 5. Load beta[b, h, 0..T-1] into shared memory 6. __syncthreads() 7. Each thread j loops over T, reading from shared memory 8. Write results to global memory This eliminates all global memory reads in the loop! The only global memory access is: - One-time load of inputs into shared memory - Write of outputs per timestep But we only have 32 threads per block. Can 32 threads load 25.5 KB efficiently? - 25.5 KB / 32 threads = 816 bytes per thread - At 128 bytes per coalesced transaction, that's ~7 transactions per thread - Very doable in a few microseconds But wait, the writes to output are still global memory. We write D floats per timestep = 96 bytes per timestep per block. Over T=128 timesteps, that's 12.3 KB per block. Still needs to go to global memory. Could we accumulate outputs in shared memory and write once at the end? - Output shape: [T, D] = 128 * 24 * 4 = 12.3 KB - We need one output per timestep, not just one at the end - Actually the reference computes and returns output for ALL timesteps - So we must write the full [T, D] output - 12.3 KB per block, 8 blocks = 98.6 KB total writes So the shared memory approach might help by reducing repeated global memory reads of q, k, v, gate, beta. But in the original reference, q, k, v, gate, beta are also read from memory repeatedly (though they might be cached by PyTorch's loop). Actually, in the original Python loop, at each iteration, it slices q[:, t], which creates a view, and then does operations. PyTorch caches these tensors, so each element is read from global memory once per iteration. So the original does read all inputs T times. With the shared memory preload, we read each input exactly once from global memory. That's a huge reduction in memory traffic! Total global memory reads with preload: - q: B * H * T * D = 2 * 4 * 128 * 24 = 24,576 floats = 98.3 KB - k: same = 98.3 KB - v: same = 98.3 KB - gate: B * H * T = 1,024 floats = 4 KB - beta: same = 4 KB - Total reads: ~303 KB Without preload (reading each timestep in the loop): - Total reads = 303 KB * T reads per element... no wait - Each element is read T times, but since the loop is sequential and the cache might retain data... - Actually, with 192 blocks, each block reads q, k for one (b, h) once per timestep - Wait no, in the "1 block per (b, h)" approach, all j values are in the same block - So q[b,h,t,i] is read by thread i at timestep t - Since different timesteps t read different memory addresses, caching doesn't help much - Each float of q, k, v, gate, beta is read T times from global memory - Total global memory reads without preload: 303 KB * T = ~38 MB With preload, we reduce reads from ~38 MB to ~303 KB, a 128x reduction! That's huge. But we need enough shared memory. Let me calculate: - shmem_q: T * D floats - shmem_k: T * D floats - shmem_v: T * D floats - shmem_gate: T floats - shmem_beta: T floats - Total = (3 * T * D + 2 * T) * 4 bytes - For T=128, D=24: (3 * 128 * 24 + 2 * 128) * 4 = (9216 + 256) * 4 = 37,888 bytes = 37 KB Per SM shared memory: 228 KB. So we can fit 6 blocks per SM. With 8 blocks total, that means 2 SMs can hold all blocks, and the rest are idle. But each block executing on an SM will have its data in fast shared memory. Wait, but if multiple blocks run on the same SM concurrently, they share the SM's shared memory. On Hopper, each block gets its own shared memory partition (up to 228 KB total). So 6 blocks × 37 KB = 222 KB, which fits. With 8 blocks, if we run 4 blocks per SM on 2 SMs, that's 148 KB per SM. Fine. Or if we run 2 blocks per SM on 4 SMs, that's 74 KB per SM. Also fine. So with shared memory preload, we get much less global memory bandwidth demand, which should make the kernel faster even with few blocks. However, there's a catch: the preload itself takes time and requires all threads in the block to participate. With only 32 threads (24 active), the preload is somewhat inefficient but still much faster than T repeated global reads. Actually, for thread j, it only needs to load some portion of the data. Let me think about how to parallelize the preload: - Threads 0..23 each load a column of data - For q: thread i loads q[0..T-1, i] — T floats, strided by D - This is NOT coalesced if threads read strided memory - Better: all threads participate in loading contiguous chunks For coalesced loading: - We have 32 threads, we want them to read contiguous memory - q is stored as [B, T, H, D], so for fixed (b, h), q[t=0..T-1][i=0..D-1] is not fully contiguous because of the T dimension stride - Wait, q[b, t, h, :] has stride 1 (contiguous) - q[b, :, h, :] has stride H*D between t values So q[b, 0, h, 0..D-1] is contiguous (D floats) q[b, 1, h, 0..D-1] is at offset H*D from the previous If 32 threads read q[b, t, h, 0..31], that's 32 contiguous floats = 1 coalesced read per timestep. But we only need 24 floats per timestep. Threads 0-23 read the data, threads 24-31 are masked. For the preload loop: ```cuda for (int t = threadIdx.x; t < T; t += blockDim.x) { // Load q[b, t, h, 0..D-1] // Load k[b, t, h, 0..D-1] // Load v[b, t, h, 0..D-1] // Load gate[b, t, h] // Load beta[b, t, h] } ``` Wait, threadIdx.x ranges from 0 to 31. If threadIdx.x >= D, it's masked for the computation but can still participate in memory loads! This is the key. Actually, for the preload, having thread 24 load q[b, t+1, h, 0..D-1] might not make sense. We should have all threads load contiguous chunks of the overall data. Better approach: - Treat the per-(b,h) data as a flat array - All threads load consecutive floats - Total floats to load: 3*T*D + 2*T = 9472 floats - With 32 threads, each thread loads 9472/32 = 296 floats - This is fully coalesced ```cuda float* src = &q[((b * T) * H + h) * D]; float* dst = shmem_q; int total_floats = 3 * T * D + 2 * T; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_floats; idx += blockDim.x) { // Need to map idx to the right source tensor // Complicated because there are 5 tensors with different sizes } ``` Mapping is annoying. Simpler: preload each tensor separately. ```cuda for (int t = threadIdx.x; t < T; t += blockDim.x) { for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { shmem_q[t * D + i] = q[((b * T + t) * H + h) * D + i]; } } ``` This is bad because thread t loads all i values, and different threads load different t values. The memory accesses are strided by H*D between threads? No: - Thread 0 reads q at offset ((b*T+0)*H+h)*D + 0, then +1, +2, ... - Thread 1 reads q at offset ((b*T+1)*H+h)*D + 0, then +1, +2, ... Wait, the i loop inside makes each thread read D consecutive floats. But thread 0 and thread 1 read addresses separated by H*D floats. For H=4, D=24, that's 96 floats = 384 bytes. If blockDim.x = 32, the separation between thread 0 and thread 1 is 384 bytes, which is much larger than the warp coalescing width (128 bytes). So threads 0 and 1 read from different cache lines. But threads 0, 32, 64... all read addresses separated by 32*384 bytes. Hmm, this isn't coalesced within a warp. A warp reads 32 different cache lines, which is terrible. To make it coalesced, adjacent threads should read adjacent memory. So we want: - Thread 0 reads address A - Thread 1 reads address A+1 - Thread 2 reads address A+2 - etc. But our data for different t values is not contiguous. For fixed (b, h), q[t=0, i=0..D-1] is contiguous, then there are (H-1)*D floats of other heads, then q[t=1, i=0..D-1]. So the data per (b,h) is: D floats, then gap of (H-1)*D, then D floats, then gap, etc. If we have 32 threads, we can read 32 contiguous floats at a time. For example: - Threads 0-23 read q[t=0, i=0..23] (24 floats, contiguous) - Then threads 0-23 read q[t=1, i=0..23] (but these are not contiguous with t=0) Actually, since threads run together in a warp, if thread 0 reads q[0] and thread 1 reads q[1], they ARE coalesced. Let me just write the kernel without shared memory preload, using direct global memory access but with coalesced reads. The L2 cache should help significantly since q, k, v are read repeatedly by different blocks/warps, and for the same (b,h), q[t] is read at timestep t but not again. Actually, for the "1 block per (b,h), 1 warp per block" approach: - All threads in the warp loop over t together - At timestep t, all threads read q[t, :] and k[t, :] — these are coalesced - All threads also read v[t, j] where j = threadIdx.x — for a warp, threads 0..23 read v[t, 0..23] - Wait, v[t, :] is contiguous in memory! So threads 0..23 reading v[t, j] IS coalesced! This is beautiful! In the "1 warp per (b,h)" approach, thread j reads v[t, j], which IS coalesced because v's last dimension is contiguous. And thread j writes out[t, j], which is also coalesced. Gate and beta are read as gate[t] and beta[t], which are the same address for all threads. The hardware handles this via broadcast. So the memory pattern is already close to optimal: - q[t, i]: each thread reads all i over the inner loop — not coalesced within a warp - Wait, I said earlier that each thread handles one j and loops over all i. So thread j reads q[t, 0], q[t, 1], ..., q[t, D-1]. Different threads in the warp read the SAME q[t, i] values! That's redundant and NOT coalesced. Ah, that's a problem! In the "1 thread per j" approach, all threads in a warp read the same q[t, i] values at the same time. So 24 threads all read q[t, 0] simultaneously. This is a broadcast, which the cache can handle, but it's still 24 reads from the same address in a single instruction. The GPU might coalesce this into a single read, but I'm not sure. Similarly, all threads read k[t, i] at the same time — also broadcast. For v[t, j], thread j reads a different j — this IS coalesced. So the issue is that q and k are read redundantly by all threads. In the 192-block approach (1 block per j, threads per i), thread i reads q[t, i] and k[t, i], which IS coalesced, and only once. But then we need warp shuffle reduction. Let me compare: **Approach A: 8 blocks, 32 threads per block, each thread handles one j** - Reads q: T * D times per block (each thread reads all q values) - But all 24 active threads in the warp read q[t, i] simultaneously - The read might be cached and broadcast, so only 1 cache line fetched - Total q reads from memory: T * D per block - 8 blocks: 8 * T * D = 8 * 128 * 24 = 24,576 floats = ~98 KB - Reads k: same, ~98 KB - Reads v: T * D per block (each thread reads one v, coalesced across warp) - 8 blocks: ~98 KB - Total reads: ~294 KB + gate/beta Wait, I calculated the total input size earlier as ~303 KB. So Approach A reads roughly the total input size once (because each (b,h) block reads its portion once). That's actually very efficient! The key insight is: although 24 threads all read q[t, i] at the same time, the GPU memory system detects that they all read the same address and returns the data via the L1 cache with minimal bandwidth usage. It's essentially a broadcast read. So Approach A might already have near-optimal memory bandwidth. The time is dominated by the sequential loop over T. **Approach B: 192 blocks, 32 threads per block, each thread handles one i, for one j** - Each block reads q: T floats (one per timestep, q[t, i] for fixed i) - 192 blocks * T = 192 * 128 = 24,576 floats, same total - Each block reads k: same - Each block reads v: T floats (v[t, j] for fixed j) - 192 blocks * T = 24,576 floats - Wait, v is size B*H*T*D = 24,576 floats total - So total v reads = 24,576 floats, same as total v size - Total reads: same as Approach A! Both approaches read the same total memory. The difference is: - Approach A: 8 warps, each doing T iterations with D inner loop steps - Approach B: 192 warps, each doing T iterations with 1 inner loop step + reduction Actually, wait. So each warp does T iterations, at each iteration doing 1 state update and accumulating a partial sum. Then 5 shuffle instructions for reduction. In Approach A, each thread does D state updates and 1 dot product per timestep. Since the thread loops over i, the compiler might unroll or vectorize. A warp of 24 active threads each executing the i-loop means 24 threads doing independent loops. But since they're in the same warp, they execute in lockstep (each iteration of the i-loop is done simultaneously by all threads). Wait, in Approach A, if all threads loop over i=0..D-1, then at iteration i: - Thread j reads q[t, i], k[t, i] (same for all j) - Thread j reads v[t, j] (different for each j, but same across the i-loop) - Thread j updates state[i] - Thread j accumulates q[t, i] * state[i] into a running sum Since all threads read the same q[t, i] and k[t, i], this is a broadcast. All threads are active, doing the same operations but on different data (their local state[j][i] and local v_j). In Approach B, per t, each thread does: - Load q[t, i] (coalesced) - Load k[t, i] (coalesced) - Load v[t, j] (broadcast, since all threads in block have same j) - Update state[i] - Accumulate q[t, i] * state[i] - 5 shuffle instructions for reduction - Store result Wait, in Approach B, each block is one (b,h,j), but has 32 threads each handling one i. The warp loops over T. Total useful work per warp is small, so execution time might be dominated by loop overhead and shuffle latency. Actually, I just realized that in Approach A, all 24 threads in the warp execute the inner i-loop in lockstep. At each i, all threads read the same q[t,i] and k[t,i]. Let me write this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void kernel(const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* gate, const float* beta, float* out, int B, int T, int H, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int j = threadIdx.x; if (j >= D) return; // Only first D threads are active // Compute base offsets int64_t base_qkv = ((int64_t)b * T * H + h) * D; int64_t base_gate = (int64_t)b * T * H + h; int64_t stride_t = (int64_t)H * D; int64_t stride_gate = H; float state[24]; // For D=24; for general D, we'd need dynamic allocation #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) state[i] = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < T; t++) { int64_t offset = (int64_t)t * stride_t; int64_t offset_gate = (int64_t)t * stride_gate; float g = gate[base_gate + offset_gate]; float b_t = beta[base_gate + offset_gate]; float v_j = v[base_qkv + offset + j]; float accum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { float q_i = q[base_qkv + offset + i]; float k_i = k[base_qkv + offset + i]; state[i] = g * state[i] + b_t * k_i * v_j; accum += q_i * state[i]; } out[base_qkv + offset + j] = accum; } } ``` Wait, `state[24]` with a compile-time constant works only if D <= 24. Optimize the inner loop with manual unrolling or vectorization 4. Maybe preload some data Actually, let me think about this from a different angle. What if I compute the recurrence differently? State update: S_t = g_t * S_{t-1} + b_t * k_t ⊗ v_t Output: y_t = q_t · S_t We can write this as: For each (b, h, t): For each j: For each i: S_{t,i,j} = g_t * S_{t-1,i,j} + b_t * k_{t,i} * v_{t,j} y_{t,j} = sum_i q_{t,i} * S_{t,i,j} This is exactly what we have. We can reorganize the computation order for better locality: For each (b, h, t): gv = g_t (scalar) bv = b_t (scalar) For each i: delta_i = bv * k_{t,i} For each j: S_{t,i,j} = gv * S_{t-1,i,j} + delta_i * v_{t,j} For each j: y_{t,j} = sum_i q_{t,i} * S_{t,i,j} This is the same but with different loop order. It doesn't help because S_{t,i,j} for different j are independent, and for the output we need all i for each j. Wait, what if we compute the delta-rule update as an outer product accumulation: S_t = g_t * S_{t-1} + b_t * outer(k_t, v_t) Then y_t = q_t · S_t The outer product creates a full D×D matrix each timestep. In the reference, this is explicitly computed. In our kernel, we avoid creating the full matrix by updating state incrementally. But what if we use shared memory to hold the full D×D state for one (b,h), and have all threads in a block update it collaboratively? - Block = one (b, h) - Block size = D threads (24) - At each t: - Thread i computes delta_i = b_t * k_{t,i} and stores in shared memory - Thread j reads v_{t,j} (already in register or shared) - Thread (i,j) updates S[i,j] = g_t * S[i,j] + delta_i * v_j - But we only have D threads, not D×D We could have each thread handle one i, and update all j values for that i: - Thread i: for j=0..D-1, S[i,j] = g_t * S[i,j] + delta_i * v_j - Then compute y_j = sum_i q_i * S[i,j] for all j - But each thread i computes its contribution to y_j: contrib_i_j = q_i * S[i,j] - We need to sum over i: y_j = sum_i contrib_i_j - This is a reduction over i for each j With D threads, thread i can compute contrib_i_j for all j and add to a shared memory accumulator: ```cuda __shared__ float y[D]; for (int j = 0; j < D; j++) { S[i][j] = g * S[i][j] + delta_i * v[j]; atomicAdd(&y[j], q[i] * S[i][j]); } ``` But atomics are slow. Better to use parallel reduction. Alternatively: - Thread j computes y_j = sum_i q_i * S[i][j] - But S[i][j] for all i are stored in different threads' states - So we'd need to transpose: store S in shared memory with layout [D][D], then have thread j read S[0..D-1][j] If we store S in shared memory [D][D] = 24*24 = 576 floats = 2.3 KB. Very small. Then the computation can be: 1. Load q[t], k[t], v[t], gate, beta into shared memory or registers 2. Thread i updates S[i][0..D-1] in shared memory 3. __syncthreads() 4. Thread j computes y_j = sum_i q[i] * S[i][j] 5. Write y_j Wait, step 2: Thread i needs to update S[i][j] for all j. If S is in shared memory, each thread writes D floats. With D=24, that's 24 writes per thread per timestep. Then step 4: each thread reads D floats (column j of S). That's 24 reads per thread per timestep. Total shared memory traffic per timestep: D threads * D writes + D threads * D reads = 2*D² = 1152 floats = 4.6 KB. At ~10 TB/s shared memory bandwidth, that's ~0.5 ns. Very fast. And we avoid per-thread register arrays entirely! The state is in shared memory, shared by all threads in the block. But the issue is still: only 24 threads per block, and 8 blocks total. Same parallelism as before. However, this "shared state" approach has a nice property: the state is persistent in shared memory across timesteps, so we don't need registers. And the memory pattern is very regular. Let me think about whether this is faster than the per-thread register approach. In the register approach: - Each thread has state[24] in registers - At each t, each thread does 24 updates and 24 multiply-adds for the dot product - All operations use registers (very fast) - No sync needed In the shared memory approach: - State is in shared memory [24][24] - At each t, thread i reads S[i][0..23] (24 reads), computes new values, writes back (24 writes) - __syncthreads() - Thread j reads S[0..23][j] (24 reads), computes dot product with q - Total shared memory ops per thread per t: 48 reads/writes - Register approach: 24 reads/writes to registers (implicit) + a few temporaries Registers are faster than shared memory, so the register approach is probably faster. The shared memory approach has the overhead of __syncthreads() twice per timestep. I'll stick with the register approach. One final idea: what if we process multiple timesteps within a single thread, using the fact that after T steps, S_T can be written as: S_T = (Π_t g_t) S_0 + Σ_t (β_t * k_t ⊗ v_t * Π_{s>t} g_s) If we precompute prefix products of g and suffix products of g, each output y_t could be computed from a closed form. But this requires: 1. Computing prefix products: P_t = g_0 * g_1 * ... * g_t 2. Computing suffix products: Q_t = g_t * g_{t+1} * ... * g_{T-1} 3. Then S_t = P_t * S_0 + Σ_{s=0..t} (β_s * k_s ⊗ v_s * P_t / P_s) This is interesting! The state S_t can be expressed as: S_t = P_t * S_0 + Σ_{s=0..t} (β_s * k_s ⊗ v_s * P_t / P_s) = P_t * [S_0 + Σ_{s=0..t} (β_s * k_s ⊗ v_s / P_s)] Let R_s = β_s / P_s. Then: S_t = P_t * [S_0 + Σ_{s=0..t} (R_s * k_s ⊗ v_s)] And y_t = q_t · S_t = P_t * q_t · [S_0 + Σ_{s=0..t} (R_s * k_s ⊗ v_s)] This separates into: 1. Compute prefix products P_t = Π_{s=0..t} g_s (T values) 2. Compute R_s = β_s / P_s (T values) 3. Compute cumulative outer products C_t = Σ_{s=0..t} R_s * k_s ⊗ v_s - This is a cumulative sum of D×D matrices - C_t = C_{t-1} + R_t * k_t ⊗ v_t 4. Then y_t = P_t * q_t · C_t Steps 1 and 2 are just scans over T. Step 3 is a scan over T of D×D matrices. Step 4 is just matrix-vector multiplies. But step 3 still requires a sequential scan over T because each C_t depends on C_{t-1}. We can't parallelize the scan easily because the "state" is D×D. However, we CAN parallelize it if we use a parallel scan algorithm, but the cross-thread communication of a D×D matrix would be extremely expensive. Alternatively, if we process step 3 sequentially but in CUDA (one thread per (b,h), looping over t), we get the same parallelism as before but with a slightly different arithmetic structure. The main benefit is that the computation is reorganized, but I'm not sure it's faster. Actually, the math suggests: - Original: at each t, we do D² multiplies and D² adds for state update, plus D² multiplies and D adds for output. - Reorganized: at each t, we do one divide for R_t, one outer product (D² multiplies), one matrix add (D² adds), and D² multiplies + D adds for output. - So about the same amount of compute. And the state is still D×D, whether we call it S_t or C_t. No real gain. Back to the original loop. One more idea: we can compute outputs for all timesteps simultaneously by doing a parallel associative scan on 1×1 "matrices" for each (i,j) pair individually. For each (b, h, i, j): S_t[i,j] = g_t * S_{t-1}[i,j] + β_t * k_t[i] * v_t[j] This is a scalar linear recurrence! For each (i,j), we can do a parallel scan over t. Wait, but we need y_t[j] = sum_i q_t[i] * S_t[i,j]. This means for each t and j, we need to sum over i. If we compute all S_t[i,j] in parallel, we'd still need a reduction over i for each (t, j). Alternatively: 1. Actually, using `for (int i = 0; i < D; i++)` where D is a template parameter <24>, the compiler knows D=24 and can fully unroll the loop. This is ideal. Let me write the kernel with template D. ```cuda template <int D> __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ out, int64_t B, int64_t T, int64_t H) { int64_t b = blockIdx.x; int64_t h = blockIdx.y; int64_t j = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B || h >= H || j >= D) return; int64_t stride_t = H * D; int64_t base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D; int64_t base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; float state[D]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { state[i] = 0.0f; } for (int64_t t = 0; t < T; t++) { int64_t offset = t * stride_t; int64_t offset_gate = t * H; float g = gate[base_gate + offset_gate]; float b_val = beta[base_gate + offset_gate]; float v_j = v[base_qkv + offset + j]; float accum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { float q_i = q[base_qkv + offset + i]; float k_i = k[base_qkv + offset + i]; state[i] = g * state[i] + b_val * k_i * v_j; accum += q_i * state[i]; } out[base_qkv + offset + j] = accum; } } ``` For the host wrapper, we'd dispatch based on D: ```c++ void scalar_gated_delta_attention( torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v, torch::Tensor gate, torch::Tensor beta, torch::Tensor out) { int64_t B = q.size(0); int64_t T = q.size(1); int64_t H = q.size(2); int64_t D = q.size(3); dim3 grid(B, H); dim3 block(D); switch (D) { case 8: scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<8><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; case 16: scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<16><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; case 24: scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<24><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; case 32: scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<32><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; case 64: scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<64><<<grid, block>>>(...); break; default: // Generic fallback scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_generic<<<grid, block>>>(..., D); break; } } ``` The generic fallback would use dynamic allocation or a large fixed-size array. But for the benchmark, D=24, so we'd hit the template path. Wait, but block size = D = 24. Is a block with 24 threads allowed? Yes, CUDA allows any block size from 1 to 1024. The warp has 24 active threads and 8 inactive threads. The warp scheduler dispatches the warp, and the 8 inactive threads are simply not present. Let me design this version: ```cuda // One block per (b, h) // Block size: WARP_SIZE * D = 32 * 24 = 768 threads // Warp j handles j = warp_id (0..23) // Within warp j, lane i handles i = lane_id (0..23) // Lanes 24..31 are masked/inactive __global__ void kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; // 0..23 int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; // 0..31 if (warp_id >= D) return; // Only first D warps active if (lane_id >= D) { // Lanes >= D don't do useful work but participate in shuffle // They'll compute partial = 0 for the reduction } int j = warp_id; int i = lane_id; float state = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < T; t++) { int64_t offset = t * H * D; int64_t offset_gate = t * H; float g = gate[base_gate + offset_gate]; float b_val = beta[base_gate + offset_gate]; float v_j = v[base_qkv + offset + j]; float q_i = (i < D) ? q[base_qkv + offset + i] : 0.0f; float k_i = (i < D) ? k[base_qkv + offset + i] : 0.0f; state = g * state + b_val * k_i * v_j; float partial = (i < D) ? q_i * state : 0.0f; // Warp shuffle reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { out[base_qkv + offset + j] = partial; } } } ``` Wait, but in this design, all 32 lanes in a warp execute together. Lanes 24-31 compute q_i=0, k_i=0, partial=0, state updates to g*state (since k_i=0). So lanes 24-31 unnecessarily update their state! That's a bug. If lane i >= D, it should NOT update its state, or its state should be 0 throughout. But in the reduction, it contributes 0 to the sum, which is fine. However, the state variable for lanes 24-31 is still being updated in the loop, which is wasted work but doesn't affect correctness. Wait, does it affect correctness? Lane i=24 computes state = g * state + b * 0 * v_j = g * state. Starting from state=0, it stays 0. So state remains 0. No problem. Actually, `state = g * state + b_val * k_i * v_j` where k_i = 0 and state starts at 0. So state stays 0. The arithmetic is wasted but correct. Then `partial = q_i * state = 0 * 0 = 0`. Contribution to reduction is 0. Correct. So lanes 24-31 do useless work but don't affect correctness. Great. But now consider: each warp has 24 active lanes doing real work and 8 idle lanes. Memory reads per block per timestep: - q: D floats loaded coalesced across 24 active lanes = 1-2 memory transactions - k: same - v: 1 float broadcast to all 32 lanes = 1 memory transaction (cached) - gate: 1 float broadcast = cached - beta: 1 float broadcast = cached - out: 1 float write per warp = 1 memory transaction Total per block per timestep: ~4-5 memory transactions. Actually, I realize that with 24 warps per block, all warps execute the same loop over t, but they access different j values. At a given t, all 24 warps are at the same instruction (since they're in the same block, executing together). So 24 warps simultaneously access v[t, j] for j=0..23. Since v[t, :] is contiguous, these 24 reads are contiguous and should coalesce into 1-2 memory transactions! That's very efficient. Similarly, for the output write, all 24 warps write out[t, j] for j=0..23 at the same time. These writes are also coalesced into 1-2 transactions. Meanwhile, q[t, i] and k[t, i] are read within each warp. For warp j, lane i reads q[t, i]. So within each warp, the 24 active lanes read q[t, 0..23], which is coalesced. Since all 24 warps do this simultaneously, we have 24 warps each doing one coalesced read of 24 floats. But the reads are for the SAME addresses across all warps! So it's 24 identical coalesced reads hitting the same cache lines. The L1 cache handles this by broadcasting the data to all warps. Total memory traffic is just the cache line size per read. This is extremely efficient! The 8-block, 24-warp-per-block design has excellent memory coalescing and cache utilization. OK, I'm convinced. Let me write this kernel. Let me write the final code: ```cpp template <int D> __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_small( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ out, int64_t B, int64_t T, int64_t H) { int64_t b = blockIdx.x; int64_t h = blockIdx.y; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; // threadIdx.x / 32 int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31; // threadIdx.x % 32 if (b >= B || h >= H || warp_id >= D) return; int64_t stride_t = H * D; int64_t base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D; int64_t base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; int j = warp_id; float state = 0.0f; for (int64_t t = 0; t < T; t++) { int64_t offset = t * stride_t; int64_t offset_gate = t * H; float g = gate[base_gate + offset_gate]; float b_val = beta[base_gate + offset_gate]; float v_j = v[base_qkv + offset + j]; float q_i = (lane_id < D) ? q[base_qkv + offset + lane_id] : 0.0f; float k_i = (lane_id < D) ? k[base_qkv + offset + lane_id] : 0.0f; state = g * state + b_val * k_i * v_j; float partial = (lane_id < D) ? q_i * state : 0.0f; // Warp shuffle reduction #pragma unroll for (int shfl_offset = 16; shfl_offset > 0; shfl_offset >>= 1) { partial += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, shfl_offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { out[base_qkv + offset + j] = partial; } } } ``` Wait, I just realized there's a subtle issue. The state for each lane is independent. For lane i, state holds S[i, j]. The update is: `state = g * state + b_val * k_i * v_j` This is correct. At t=0, state=0. After first iteration: `state = g_0 * 0 + b_0 * k_0[i] * v_0[j] = b_0 * k_0[i] * v_0[j]` Correct. And `partial = q_i * state` where state = S_t[i, j]. Then we sum over i to get y_t[j] = sum_i q_t[i] * S_t[i, j]. Correct. Great, the logic is sound. One concern: the warp shuffle reduction sums all 32 lanes. For lanes 24-31, partial = 0. So the sum is correct. But `__shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, 16)` sums lanes that are 16 apart. For lanes 0-15, they get partial from lanes 16-31. Those are 0. So after offset=16: partial[0..15] += 0, partial[16..23] += 0. No change for useful lanes. After offset=8: lane 0 gets lane 8, lane 1 gets lane 9, etc. Useful. After offset=4: useful. After offset=2: useful. After offset=1: useful. Total: 5 shuffle-adds. The last one for lanes 23 pairs with lane 22, and lane 23 gets the sum of lanes 22 and 23. Then lane 22 gets the sum of lanes 22 and 23. Wait no, lane 22 and 23 are exchanging. After offset=1, lane 22 += lane 23, lane 23 += lane 22. So both lanes 22 and 23 have the same partial sum (of lanes 22 and 23). But we only need lane 0 to have the full sum. Actually, in a full warp shuffle tree reduction, after all steps, ALL lanes have the full sum. Let me verify: - After offset=16: lane i += lane i^16. Lanes 0-15 get lanes 16-31 (0). Lanes 16-31 get lanes 0-15. But lanes 16-23 get useful values, lanes 24-31 get 0. - After offset=8: lane i += lane i^8. - Lane 0 += lane 8 - Lane 1 += lane 9 - ... - Lane 8 += lane 0 - ... - Lane 16 += lane 24 (0) - Lane 17 += lane 25 (0) - ... - Lane 24 += lane 16 - ... - After multiple steps, the values propagate. After all 5 steps, lane 0 will have the sum of lanes 0..23 because: - offset=16: lane 0 += lane 16 - offset=8: lane 0 += lane 8 - offset=4: lane 0 += lane 4 - offset=2: lane 0 += lane 2 - offset=1: lane 0 += lane 1 This accumulates lanes 0, 1, 2, 4, 8, 16. But what about lane 3? After offset=2, lane 2 += lane 3 and lane 3 += lane 2. So lane 2 has lanes 2+3. Then at offset=1, lane 0 += lane 1 (which has lanes 0+1). Wait, I need to trace more carefully. Actually, the standard shuffle-xor reduction over a full warp works as follows, assuming all lanes are active: Initial: lane i has val_i Step 16: lane i = val_i + val_{i^16} Step 8: lane i = (val_i + val_{i^16}) + (val_{i^8} + val_{i^8^16}) Step 4: lane i accumulates 4 more values After step 1: lane i has sum of all 32 lanes. Yes, because each xor creates a binary tree that eventually covers all bits. After 5 steps, every lane has the sum of all 32 lanes. So lane 0 will have the sum of lanes 0..31. Since lanes 24..31 are 0, lane 0 gets sum of lanes 0..23. Correct. And we only need lane 0, which has the correct sum. Perfect. Now, there's one more issue: the `if (lane_id == 0)` check after the reduction. In CUDA, divergent branches within a warp are handled via masking. So only lane 0 executes the write. That's fine. But wait, all 32 threads in the warp are active for lane_id < D. Actually, warp_id >= D causes early return, but for warp_id < D, all 32 lanes execute the t-loop. Lanes 24-31 compute partial=0 and don't affect the reduction. Is there a performance penalty for having lanes 24-31 execute the full loop with dummy calculations? The loop body is small, and their state stays 0. The arithmetic is cheap. The main cost is that the warp executes for all 32 lanes, taking the same time as a warp with 24 active lanes plus 8 dummy lanes. There's essentially no penalty because the warp executes as a unit regardless. OK great. Let me now write the Python code that loads this kernel. Actually, the generic small D kernel is exactly the same as the templated one in terms of logic. The only difference is D is a runtime parameter instead of compile-time constant. For the inner loop `for (int i = 0; i < D; i++)`, if D is runtime, the compiler can't unroll it. But we don't have an inner loop in the warp-shuffle version! In the warp-shuffle version, each lane handles one i. The "inner loop" is across lanes, not within a thread. So the per-thread code doesn't depend on D (except for the bounds check `lane_id < D`). Wait, the warp-shuffle version doesn't have a loop over i at all! Each lane handles one i. The "loop over i" is implicit in the warp parallelism. This means the generic kernel is almost identical to the templated one! ```cuda __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_small_generic( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ out, int64_t B, int64_t T, int64_t H, int D) { int64_t b = blockIdx.x; int64_t h = blockIdx.y; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31; if (b >= B || h >= H || warp_id >= D) return; int64_t stride_t = H * D; int64_t base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D; int64_t base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; int j = warp_id; float state = 0.0f; for (int64_t t = 0; t < T; t++) { int64_t offset = t * stride_t; int64_t offset_gate = t * H; float g = gate[base_gate + offset_gate]; float b_val = beta[base_gate + offset_gate]; float v_j = v[base_qkv + offset + j]; float q_i = (lane_id < D) ? q[base_qkv + offset + lane_id] : 0.0f; float k_i = (lane_id < D) ? k[base_qkv + offset + lane_id] : 0.0f; state = g * state + b_val * k_i * v_j; float partial = (lane_id < D) ? q_i * state : 0.0f; for (int shfl_offset = 16; shfl_offset > 0; shfl_offset >>= 1) { partial += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, shfl_offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { out[base_qkv + offset + j] = partial; } } } ``` This generic kernel works for any D and is very similar to the templated one. The only difference is the `for` loop over `shfl_offset`, which is unrollable anyway. So the generic small D kernel is actually the SAME code as the templated one, just without the template parameter. This means we don't really need the template at all! Let me write that generic kernel: ```cuda __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_large( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ out, int64_t B, int64_t T, int64_t H, int D) { int64_t b = blockIdx.x; int64_t h = blockIdx.y; int j = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B || h >= H || j >= D) return; int64_t stride_t = H * D; int64_t base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D; int64_t base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; // For large D, we need a dynamic array. We'll use a fixed max size with a check. // Actually, better to use shared memory for the state. } ``` For large D, maintaining a state array per thread in registers might spill to local memory. For D=64, block size = 64 = 2 warps. For D=128, 4 warps. This is already better occupancy than D=24 (1 warp). For the "large D" kernel, each thread handles one j and loops over i: ```cuda float* state = (float*)alloca(D * sizeof(float)); for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) state[i] = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < T; t++) { float g = gate[...]; float b_val = beta[...]; float v_j = v[...]; float accum = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { float q_i = q[...]; float k_i = k[...]; state[i] = g * state[i] + b_val * k_i * v_j; accum += q_i * state[i]; } out[...] = accum; } ``` The `alloca` might not work in CUDA. We can just use `float state[256];` if we assume D <= 256. Or we can increase to `state[512]`. For very large D, we might need dynamic allocation in shared memory: ```cuda extern __shared__ float s_state[]; // s_state is sized as blockDim.x * D floats float* state = &s_state[threadIdx.x * D]; ``` This uses shared memory, which is slower than registers but faster than local memory. And it allows any D. But for simplicity, I'll assume D <= 128 and use `float state[128]` in the large kernel. For D > 32: use the per-thread register version with block size = D (or min(D, 1024)), handling all j values per block This gives good performance for the benchmark (D=24) and reasonable performance for larger D. Let me finalize this design. For D <= 32 kernel (warp shuffle): ```cuda __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_small(...) ``` For D > 32 kernel (per-thread state): ```cuda __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_large(...) ``` In the host wrapper: ```cpp void scalar_gated_delta_attention(...) { int D = q.size(3); dim3 grid(B, H); if (D <= 32) { int chunks = (D + 7) / 8; dim3 grid(B, H, chunks); dim3 block(256); scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_small<<<grid, block>>>(..., D); } else { dim3 block(min(D, 1024)); dim3 grid(B, H, (D + block.x - 1) / block.x); // Wait, if block.x = D, we need only grid(B, H) // But we might want multiple blocks per (b,h) for better occupancy // Actually for D > 32, a block of D threads is at least 1 warp, maybe more // Let's keep it simple: grid(B, H), block(min(D, 1024)) // If D > 1024, use multiple blocks per (b,h) in z dimension if (D <= 1024) { dim3 block(D); scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_large<<<dim3(B, H), block>>>(..., D); } else { int j_per_block = 1024; dim3 block(j_per_block); dim3 grid(B, H, (D + j_per_block - 1) / j_per_block); scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_large<<<grid, block>>>(..., D); } } } ``` For the large kernel, each thread handles one j and loops over i: ```cuda __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel_large(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int j_chunk = blockIdx.z; int j = j_chunk * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (b >= B || h >= H || j >= D) return; // State array - for large D, use shared memory or rely on compiler // With D > 32, a register array might spill to local memory // Let's use a fixed-size array and hope it fits float state[128]; // max for this path... but D might be 1024! // We'd need dynamic allocation } ``` Actually, for the large D path, maintaining a state array in registers doesn't scale. Let me use shared memory for the state. But shared memory is shared among all threads in the block. If each thread needs D floats of private state, we'd need blockDim.x * D floats of shared memory. For D=1024 and block size=1024, that's 1024 * 1024 * 4 = 4 MB per block. Way too much. For large D, it's better to have each thread maintain its state in local memory (which the compiler might spill to local memory, backed by L1 cache). Or we can use a global memory buffer for the state. Actually, for the benchmark D=24, I don't need to worry about large D. Actually, in CUDA, if a block has fewer than 32 threads, those threads still form a partial warp, and the warp scheduler dispatches the full warp with some lanes inactive. The difference is that the inactive lanes are not part of the block, so they don't use register file space or thread slots. But the warp still executes as a 32-wide unit, with the extra lanes simply masked off. So from a performance perspective, launching a block with 24 threads vs 32 threads is essentially the same: the warp still takes the same time to execute. The only difference might be register allocation: with 24 threads, the block allocates registers for 24 threads, not 32. Total blocks = 8 * 4 = 32 blocks. Each block has 6 warps. With 32 blocks, each SM could run 1 block (6 warps) or multiple. Better block count than 24, but fewer warps per block. What about using `__shfl_sync` instead of `__shfl_xor_sync`? `__shfl_sync` (or `__shfl_down_sync`) can do a tree reduction that only accumulates to lane 0: ```cuda for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } ``` Wait, `__shfl_down_sync` works like: lane i gets value from lane i+offset. - After offset=16: lane 0 += lane 16, lane 1 += lane 17, etc. - After offset=8: lane 0 += lane 8, etc. - After offset=1: lane 0 += lane 1. - At the end, lane 0 has the sum of lanes 0..31. The CUDA docs say that `__shfl_down_sync` requires a convergent set of threads. If it's inside an `if` block, only some threads execute it, which violates the convergence requirement. The `__shfl_*` intrinsics can be called from within conditional code as long as all threads in the warp participate in the shuffle or have exited. But if only a subset of threads execute the shuffle, it's undefined behavior. From the CUDA C Programming Guide: > "The __shfl_*sync() intrinsics are available when the compute capability is 6.1 or higher. The mask argument indicates which threads in the warp should participate in the shuffle. Each calling thread must have its own bit set in the mask and all non-exited threads named in mask must execute the same intrinsic with the same mask, or the result is undefined." So ALL threads in the warp must execute `__shfl_down_sync`. If I put it inside `if (lane_id < D)`, then threads with lane_id >= D do NOT execute it, which is undefined behavior! To fix this, I must ensure all threads in the warp execute the shuffle. So the shuffle must be outside the `if` block. But I can safely put the memory reads inside the `if` block, and then reconverge before the shuffle. The partial for threads outside the `if` should be 0. The kernel is correct but likely suboptimal. Let me think about how to optimize it further. Current issues: 1. My kernel only computes one entry (b, h, j) of output per block, with each thread computing a single i dimension. There are B*H*D blocks total. 2. Each thread maintains its own scalar state S. But V_j is shared across all threads in a block, and gate/beta are shared too. 3. Each warp does T iterations, with 2 __syncthreads() per iteration. That's very heavy synchronization overhead. For H100, we have many SMs (132 SMs on H100 SXM). With B=2, H=4, D=24, that's only 192 blocks. That might be enough to saturate some SMs but not all. Let me think about a better approach: **Shared memory state approach:** - Each block processes one (b, h). It handles all j output dimensions. - We could load the full state matrix S[D][D] into shared memory (24*24*4 = 2304 bytes). That's small. - Then each thread can compute one (i,j) pair or one column etc. But wait, the recurrence is: S_t[i][j] = g_t * S_{t-1}[i][j] + b_t * k_t[i] * v_t[j] Then output_y_t[j] = sum_i q_t[i] * S_t[i][j] One block per (b, h): - Use a grid of (B, H) blocks. - Each block loads state S[i][j] into shared memory (D*D floats = 2304 B). - Process T time steps sequentially. - For each t: - Load gate_t, beta_t (shared) - Load k_t[i] to shared memory (D floats) - Load v_t[j] (broadcast or each thread loads its own) - Load q_t[i] to shared memory (D floats) Actually, each thread could be responsible for one or more (i, j) pairs. With D=24, we have 576 state elements. A block of 576 threads? That's above the 1024 limit. A block of 128 threads would work, each handling ~4.5 elements. But simpler: each thread handles one (i, j) pair. We need 576 threads per block if we want to cover all pairs. But max is 1024, so 576 works. Alternative: each thread handles one j (value dimension), and computes all i for that j. That's 24 threads per block, which is very small. Another option: each thread handles one i, and there are 24 threads. Each computes S[i][j] for all j. But then for the output, each thread j needs sum_i q[i] * S[i][j]. With only 24 threads, each thread could handle one j, but the S matrix needs to be transposed or accessed carefully. Actually the best approach for a single block per (b,h): - Have 24*24 = 576 threads in the block. - Each thread computes one (i, j) element of the state at each timestep. - Store S[D][D] in shared memory (or registers). - But then for the output, we need sum_i q[i] * S[i][j]. Thread (i, j) could compute partial product q[i]*S[i][j], then do a reduction across i for each j. Wait, each thread (i, j) maintains S[i][j]: `S = g*S + b*k[i]*v[j]` Then the partial contribution to out[j] is q[i]*S. We can store these partial contributions in shared memory and then have one thread per j do the reduction. But if each thread stores S in a register, and writes `s_val[j*D + i] = q[i]*S` to shared memory, then we need D*D shared memory, and a reduction of D elements for each j. With 576 threads and max 1024, that's possible but there's a lot of redundant shared memory usage. Actually, even simpler: each warp (32 threads) handles one j. Actually, another way to think about it: the current kernel computes the full S recurrence correctly, but the output is written per timestep by summing S*q. The reference computes this per timestep. Could we compute the full output more efficiently with matrix operations? Let me see if there's a mathematical simplification. S_t = g_t * S_{t-1} + b_t * k_t * v_t^T This is a scalar first-order linear recurrence on a matrix. The output is q_t^T * S_t. For each head, this is: S_t = g_t * S_{t-1} + b_t * k_t * v_t^T y_t = S_t^T * q_t (since y_t[j] = sum_i q_t[i] * S_t[i][j]) Actually y_t = (q_t^T * S_t)^T.
