Let me try a much more parallel kernel design: **Design 2: Block per (b, h), warp per j.** - Grid: (B, H) - Block: 32 warps (1024 threads) or fewer. - Assign warp 0..(D-1) to j dimension. Each warp has 32 threads, but we only need D=24 threads per warp. - Thread k in warp j handles dimension i = k % D. If k >= D, it's inactive. - Each thread maintains S[i][j] in a register. - For each timestep t: - Load gate, beta to shared memory (or all threads broadcast) - Load k_t[i] for all i to shared memory - Load v_t[j] for all j to shared memory (or each warp loads its own v_j) - Load q_t[i] for all i to shared memory - Each thread computes: S = g * S + b * k[i] * v[j] - Each thread computes partial = q[i] * S - Each warp does a reduction of 24 partials to get out[j] - Thread 0 of each warp writes out[j] This avoids __syncthreads() entirely within the timestep loop, using only warp-level reductions! Wait, can we do this? Each warp has 32 threads. We need to reduce 24 values. The first 24 threads have valid partials, threads 24-31 are inactive (or participate with 0). A warp reduction using __shfl_down_sync: float sum = partial; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); This sums all 32 threads. If threads 24-31 have 0, it works fine. So for D=24: - Grid: (B, H) - Block: 32 * D = 768 threads (24 warps) - Threads in warp j handle (i = threadIdx.x % 32, j = threadIdx.x / 32) - i ranges 0..31, but only i < D are active. Shared memory needed per block: - k_t[24], v_t[24], q_t[24], gate, beta - That's about 24*4*3 + 4*2 = 296 bytes. Tiny. Let me draft this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int j = warp_id; // Only valid if j < D and lane < D if (j >= D) return; int D_active = D; // 24 bool active = (lane < D_active); // Shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_k = smem; // size D float* s_q = smem + D; // size D float* s_v = smem + 2*D; // size D int64_t base = ((b * T + 0) * H + h) * D; int64_t base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; float S = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < T; ++t) { // Cooperatively load k_t, q_t, v_t if (tid < D) { s_k[tid] = k[base + tid]; s_q[tid] = q[base + tid]; s_v[tid] = v[base + tid]; } __syncthreads(); float g = gate[base_gate]; float b_t = beta[base_gate]; if (active) { float k_i = s_k[lane]; float v_j = s_v[j]; float q_i = s_q[lane]; S = g * S + b_t * k_i * v_j; float partial = S * q_i; // Warp reduction for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } if (lane == 0) { out[base + j] = partial; } } __syncthreads(); base += H * D; base_gate += H; } } ``` This uses one __syncthreads() per timestep to load shared memory, and then at the end to make sure all warps finish before updating base. So the kernel without shared memory: ```cuda __global__ void kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int j = warp_id; if (j >= D) return; if (lane >= D) return; // threads with lane >= D are inactive int64_t base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D; int64_t base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; int64_t stride_t = H * D; int64_t stride_gate = H; float S = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < T; ++t) { int64_t off = t * stride_t; int64_t off_g = t * stride_gate; float g = gate[base_gate + off_g]; float b_t = beta[base_gate + off_g]; float k_i = k[base_qkv + off + lane]; float v_j = v[base_qkv + off + j]; float q_i = q[base_qkv + off + lane]; S = g * S + b_t * k_i * v_j; float partial = S * q_i; // Warp reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } if (lane == 0) { out[base_qkv + off + j] = partial; } } } ``` This is extremely clean: - Grid: (B, H) = (2, 4) = 8 blocks - Block: 32 * D = 768 threads (24 warps) - No shared memory needed! - No __syncthreads() in the timestep loop! - Very lightweight. So for a 24-thread reduction: ``` sum += __shfl_down_sync(0x00FFFFFF, sum, 16); // lanes 0..7 get sum[16..23] // lanes 8..23 get their own sum (source 24..39 -> out of range) sum += __shfl_down_sync(0x00FFFFFF, sum, 8); // lanes 0..15 get sum[8..23] // lanes 16..23 get their own sum sum += __shfl_down_sync(0x00FFFFFF, sum, 4); sum += __shfl_down_sync(0x00FFFFFF, sum, 2); sum += __shfl_down_sync(0x00FFFFFF, sum, 1); ``` After step 1 (offset=16): - lane 0: sum0 + sum16 - lane 1: sum1 + sum17 - ... - lane 7: sum7 + sum23 - lane 8: sum8 + sum8 = 2*sum8 - lane 9: 2*sum9 - ... - lane 23: 2*sum23 - lane 24-31: undefined (not in mask) After step 2 (offset=8): - lane 0: (sum0+sum16) + (sum8+sum8) = wrong! This doesn't give a correct reduction of 24 elements. The standard tree reduction assumes a power of 2. For non-power-of-2, we can either: 1. Pad to 32 with zeros and do a full 32-element reduction (which works correctly). 2. Use a different reduction pattern. Option 1 is much easier. We just ensure threads 24-31 contribute 0. ```cuda float partial = (lane < D) ? (S * q_i) : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } if (lane == 0 && j < D) { out[...] = partial; } ``` This is correct because partial=0 for lanes 24-31, and 32 is a power of 2. The only overhead is those 8 threads executing the loop but contributing 0, plus a slightly larger block size. Actually, let me just use this approach. It's clean and correct. Good parallelism. Actually, I just realized we can do something more clever. Since each block handles 4 j values, and the data q_t and k_t are the same for all j values in the block, we can load them cooperatively. In the warp-shuffle design, each warp independently loads q_t and k_t. But with 4 warps per block reading the same q_t and k_t, the first warp loads them to L1/L2 cache, and subsequent warps get cache hits. We can load q_t and k_t to shared memory cooperatively within the block to save bandwidth: ``` if (threadIdx.x < D) { s_q[threadIdx.x] = q[...]; s_k[threadIdx.x] = k[...]; s_v[threadIdx.x] = v[...]; } __syncthreads(); ``` Then each warp reads from shared memory. But v_t[j] is different for each warp, so we still load it either from shared memory (s_v[j]) or direct from global. With shared memory, each block does one __syncthreads() per timestep. The memory bandwidth saving might not matter much since it's cached anyway, but it could help. Without shared memory, each thread independently loads from global. With 128 threads per block, and all reading the same q_t addresses, the cache handles it well. I think I'll try **without shared memory first** for simplicity. Let me write the kernel with 48 blocks (4 warps per block). Wait, actually, let me also consider: what if the compiler auto-vectorizes loads? Global loads within a warp that access the same cache line are coalesced. Here, lanes 0..23 of a warp load q_t[0..23], which are contiguous. That's perfectly coalesced. v_t[j] is the same address for all lanes in a warp (broadcast), which is also efficient. The main loads per timestep per active thread: - q_t[i]: 1 load, coalesced within warp - k_t[i]: 1 load, coalesced - v_t[j]: 1 load, broadcast within warp (but different for each warp) - gate_t: 1 load, broadcast - beta_t: 1 load, broadcast Total global loads per warp per timestep: D for q + D for k + 1 for v + 1 for gate + 1 for beta = 2D+3 = 51 floats. Let me write the new kernel now. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.utils.cpp_extension as ext CPP_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> void scalar_gated_delta_attention( torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v, torch::Tensor gate, torch::Tensor beta, torch::Tensor out); """ CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ out, int64_t B, int64_t T, int64_t H, int64_t D) { int64_t b = blockIdx.x; int64_t h = blockIdx.y; int j_tile = blockIdx.z; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; // threadIdx.x / 32 int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; // threadIdx.x % 32 int j = j_tile * 4 + warp_id; if (j >= D) return; int64_t stride_t = H * D; int64_t base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D; int64_t stride_gate = H; int64_t base_gate = (b * T) * H + h; float S = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int64_t t = 0; t < T; ++t) { int64_t off = t * stride_t; int64_t off_g = t * stride_gate; float g = gate[base_gate + off_g]; float b_t = beta[base_gate + off_g]; float k_i = k[base_qkv + off + lane]; float v_j = v[base_qkv + off + j]; float q_i = q[base_qkv + off + lane]; S = g * S + b_t * k_i * v_j; float partial = S * q_i; if (lane >= D) partial = 0.0f; // Warp shuffle reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } if (lane == 0) { out[base_qkv + off + j] = partial; } } } void scalar_gated_delta_attention( torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v, torch::Tensor gate, torch::Tensor beta, torch::Tensor out) { int64_t B = q.size(0); int64_t T = q.size(1); int64_t H = q.size(2); int64_t D = q.size(3); int j_tiles = (D + 3) / 4; // ceil(D/4) dim3 grid(B, H, j_tiles); dim3 block(128); // 4 warps scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), gate.data_ptr<float>(), beta.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, T, H, D); } """ _cpp_ext = ext.load_inline(...) ``` Wait, but when I do `k[base_qkv + off + lane]` for lane >= D, I'm reading out of bounds! D=24, so lane=24..31 reads k[base+off+24..31], which might be valid memory (belonging to the next head or something) but is incorrect data. I need to guard this. Even though I set partial=0 for lane >= D, the memory access itself is problematic. In each block: - j = blockIdx.z - lane = threadIdx.x - Only lanes 0..D-1 are active. ```cuda if (lane >= D) return; // threads 24-31 return, whole warp still runs with divergence float S = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int t = 0; t < T; ++t) { float g = gate[...]; float b_t = beta[...]; float k_i = k[...]; float v_j = v[...]; float q_i = q[...]; S = g * S + b_t * k_i * v_j; float partial = S * q_i; // 32-thread reduction (24 active + 8 zero) #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } if (lane == 0) out[...] = partial; } ``` Wait, I still have the out-of-bounds read issue for lanes 24-31. If I put `if (lane >= D) return;` at the start, those threads return immediately, so they won't execute the loop and won't do the loads. But they also won't participate in the warp shuffle. If I use `__shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, ...)`, the returned threads are NOT in the sync mask, so the shuffle will hang! Because `__shfl_down_sync` requires all threads in the mask to participate. If some threads have returned, they're effectively in an inactive state and won't reach the sync call. So I CANNOT have threads return early when using warp shuffle. I must ensure all threads in the warp participate in the shuffle. This means: 1. All 32 threads must reach the shuffle. 2. Lanes 24-31 can contribute 0 to the reduction. 3. But lanes 24-31 must still load valid data (or 0) to compute partial. To avoid out-of-bounds reads, I can either: - Load 0 for lanes >= D: `float k_i = (lane < D) ? k[addr] : 0.0f;` - Or pad the input tensors (requires copying, slow). I'll use the conditional load. ```cuda float k_i = 0.0f, q_i = 0.0f; if (lane < D) { k_i = k[base_qkv + off + lane]; q_i = q[base_qkv + off + lane]; } float v_j = v[base_qkv + off + j]; // j < D, so this is valid float g = gate[base_gate + off_g]; float b_t = beta[base_gate + off_g]; float S = 0.0f; // Only active lanes maintain state, but since k_i=0 for inactive lanes, // S for inactive lanes will always be 0 (assuming initial S=0 and g*S + b*0 = 0) // Actually g could be anything, 0 * g = 0. So S stays 0 for inactive lanes. S = g * S + b_t * k_i * v_j; float partial = S * q_i; // = 0 for inactive lanes #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } if (lane == 0) out[...] = partial; ``` For inactive lanes (24-31), k_i = 0, q_i = 0, so S stays 0 and partial = 0. They contribute 0 to the reduction. The shuffle uses mask 0xFFFFFFFF (all lanes), which requires all 32 threads to participate. Since we don't have any early return, all threads reach the shuffle. This works! But there's a subtle issue: `v_j` is loaded by all 32 threads in the warp. Since all threads in the block have the same j (because j = blockIdx.z), and j < D, this address is valid and the same for all threads. It's a broadcast load. Fine. Now, for 192 blocks of 32 threads each: - Total warps = 192. - Each SM can hold 32 blocks = 32 warps if limited by block count, or 64 warps if limited by warp count. - With 192 blocks total and 132 SMs, most SMs get ~1.5 blocks. Each block has 32 threads = 1 warp. - So each SM runs ~1.5 warps. That's very few. Can we increase occupancy? What if each block handles 2 j values? - Grid: (B, H, D/2) = (2, 4, 12) = 96 blocks. - Block: 64 threads (2 warps). - Warp 0 handles j=blockIdx.z*2, warp 1 handles j=blockIdx.z*2+1. - Total warps = 96 * 2 = 192 (same total warps). - But each SM now gets ~96/132 = 0.73 blocks. Each block has 2 warps, so ~1.45 warps per SM. Same! What if each block handles 4 j values? - Grid: 48 blocks. - Block: 128 threads (4 warps). - Warps per SM: 48*4/132 = 1.45. Same! The total number of warps is fixed at 192 (one per j, since each j needs one warp for the reduction). To get more warps, we need to parallelize something else, like splitting each j's computation into multiple warps. But for a fixed j, the recurrence is sequential, so we can't split it. Hmm, unless we use a parallel scan for the recurrence. But as discussed, that's complicated and probably not worth it for T=128. So we're stuck with 192 warps total. With 132 SMs and 64 warps each, the maximum theoretical occupancy is 192 / (132*64) = 2.3%. This is just because the problem is small. But SM utilization isn't everything. If the kernel is memory latency bound, having 1.45 warps per SM is enough to hide some latency if the warps can be swapped. But with only 1-2 warps, there might be idle cycles. However, on H100, each SM has 4 warp schedulers, and even a single warp can keep a scheduler busy if there are enough independent instructions. The FMA has 4-cycle latency, so we'd need 4 independent FMAs in flight to saturate one scheduler. We only have 1 FMA per 4 instructions (S update), so a single warp might not be enough. With 2 warps, maybe. But again, compared to the reference which has massive Python loop and kernel launch overhead, any CUDA kernel will be orders of magnitude faster. Wait, but we're competing against a PyTorch reference. How is the reference executed? In the reference, for each t, PyTorch computes: 1. gate_t = gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-1).unsqueeze(-1) [2 GPU ops: slice + 2 unsqueezes] 2. beta_t = similar 3. kv_outer = k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2) [slice, unsqueeze, unsqueeze, bmm] 4. state = gate_t * state + beta_t * kv_outer [mul, mul, add] 5. y_t = (q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2) [slice, unsqueeze, mul, sum] Each of these is a separate PyTorch op executed as a separate CUDA kernel launch. For T=128, that's 128 * ~10 = 1280 kernel launches! Plus intermediate tensor allocations. So even the most naive fused CUDA kernel will be 100x+ faster than the reference. Let me just go with the 192-block warp-shuffle kernel. It's simple and correct. I'll add unroll pragmas. Actually, let me reconsider the conditional load: `if (lane < D)` will cause divergence within the warp. But since the inactive lanes just skip the load and proceed with k_i=0, the divergence is minimal. After the conditional, all threads converge. One more thing: I should use `__restrict__` on pointers and mark the kernel with `__launch_bounds__` to help the compiler. Let me also consider if I should change the memory layout access pattern. Currently: ``` k_i = k[base_qkv + t * stride_t + lane] ``` This is `k[b][t][h][lane]`. For fixed b, h, t, the access to q, k is contiguous across lanes. Good. For v_j: ``` v_j = v[base_qkv + t * stride_t + j] ``` This is a broadcast load within the warp (all threads in block access the same j, but different j for different warps/blocks). Between timesteps, the address increments by `stride_t = H*D`. So for t+1, the address is t*H*D + H*D = (t+1)*H*D. This is a strided access. But with 192 blocks, each doing 128 timesteps, the L2 cache should nicely capture q, k, v for all timesteps since they fit in cache. Let me calculate: for one (b, h), q has 128*24 = 3072 floats = 12KB. k and v each 12KB. Total 36KB per (b, h). For 8 (b, h), total 288KB. H100 L2 cache is 50MB. So all the data fits in L2. Great! So the kernel will mostly be L2 cache-bound, not DRAM-bound. L2 bandwidth on H100 is much higher than DRAM. Still, with 192 warps reading the same data, there's a lot of sharing. Actually, for q and k, each (b, h, t) is read by 24 warps (one per j). The first warp does an L2 read, subsequent warps get L2 hits. v is read by 1 warp per j, so no sharing for v except within a warp. For a single (b, h): - q is read 24 times (once per j) - k is read 24 times - v is read 24 times (each j once) - gate is read 24 times - beta is read 24 times Total L2 reads per (b, h): 24 * (q + k + v + gate + beta) = 24 * 36KB = 864KB. With 8 (b,h): 6.9MB of L2 traffic. At L2 bandwidth of say 10 TB/s, this is ~0.7 microseconds. Plus compute. Let me write the kernel now. Hmm, actually, I wonder if I can do even better by having each warp handle ALL j values sequentially? A warp handles one (b, h). It loops over j=0..D-1. For each j, it loops over t=0..T-1 and computes S[i] and the reduction. But the S values for different j are independent, so we could compute them all in one pass. For a single (b, h), one warp: - For each t, load g_t, b_t, k_t[i], q_t[i]. - For each j, maintain S_j[i] = g_t * S_j[i] + b_t * k_t[i] * v_t[j]. - Then for each j, compute partial_j[i] = S_j[i] * q_t[i]. - Reduce across i for each j. But a warp only has 32 threads = 32 registers for S per j. If we have D=24 j values, we'd need 24*24 = 576 S values. That's way too many for one warp. Alternatively, process j sequentially within the warp loop: ``` float S = 0.0f; for j in 0..D-1: S = 0.0f; for t in 0..T-1: S = g_t * S + b_t * k_t[i] * v_t[j]; ... ``` But then each warp handles one (b, h) with all j values. Block size = 32 threads. Total blocks = B*H = 8. 8 blocks total = 8 warps. SM utilization is terrible. So warp-per-j is the best for parallelism. Let's go with 192 blocks of 32 threads each. Here's my final kernel design: Grid: (B, H, D) Block: 32 threads Wait, can blockDim.z coordinate be used? In CUDA, blockIdx.z is the third dimension. Grid can be 3D. Yes: `dim3 grid(B, H, D); dim3 block(32);` Then: ```cuda int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int lane = threadIdx.x; ``` This gives exactly B*H*D = 192 blocks. Let me write this kernel. One more optimization: I can use shared memory for v_t? No, each block only handles one j, so v_t[j] is just one scalar per timestep. With 192 blocks, each reading 128 floats for v (one per t), that's fine. Let me add `__launch_bounds__(32)` to the kernel since each block has exactly 32 threads. This tells the compiler it can use more registers per thread. Actually, with 192 blocks and 32 threads each, and each thread using maybe 20 registers, total registers per block = 640. An SM has 64K registers. So we can fit 100 blocks per SM register-wise. But we're limited to 32 blocks per SM and 64 warps per SM. Since each block is 1 warp, we can fit 64 blocks per SM (warp-limited). But we only have 192 blocks total, distributed over 132 SMs = 1.45 blocks per SM. Register usage is not a concern. Let me finalize the code. Wait, I want to double check one thing. In my kernel, each thread maintains its own S. The S for lane >= D stays 0 because k_i=0 and q_i=0. But what about the `__shfl_down_sync`? Lanes 24-31 contribute 0 to the reduction. The result of the reduction is the sum of lanes 0..23. That's correct. But I need to make sure all threads reach `__shfl_down_sync`. Since lanes 24-31 are still in the loop (they don't return early), and they reach the shuffle with partial=0, they participate correctly. Alright, let me write the kernel. I also need to think about whether I should use a persistent block approach or something. Nah, it's too small. Let me also think about whether I can use vectorized float2 loads for q and k. For D=24, I could load 12 float2s per warp. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nimport torch.utils.cpp_extension as ext\n\nCPP_SOURCE = r\"\"\"\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n\nvoid scalar_gated_delta_attention(\n torch::Tensor q,\n torch::Tensor k,\n torch::Tensor v,\n torch::Tensor gate,\n torch::Tensor beta,\n torch::Tensor out);\n\"\"\"\n\nCUDA_SOURCE = r\"\"\"\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n\n__global__ void __launch_bounds__(32) scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ q,\n const float* __restrict__ k,\n const float* __restrict__ v,\n const float* __restrict__ gate,\n const float* __restrict__ beta,\n float* __restrict__ out,\n int B, int T, int H, int D) {\n\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int h = blockIdx.y;\n int j = blockIdx.z;\n int lane = threadIdx.x;\n\n int stride_t = H * D;\n int base_qkv = ((b * T) * H + h) * D;\n int stride_gate = H;\n int base_gate = (b * T) * H + h;\n\n float S = 0.0f;\n\n #pragma unroll 4\n for (int t = 0; t < T; ++t) {\n int off = t * stride_t;\n int off_g = t * stride_gate;\n\n float g = gate[base_gate + off_g];\n float b_t = beta[base_gate + off_g];\n float v_j = v[base_qkv + off + j];\n\n float k_i = 0.0f;\n float q_i = 0.0f;\n if (lane < D) {\n k_i = k[base_qkv + off + lane];\n q_i = q[base_qkv + off + lane];\n }\n\n S = g * S + b_t * k_i * v_j;\n float partial = S * q_i;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset);\n }\n\n if (lane == 0) {\n out[base_qkv + off + j] = partial;\n }\n }\n}\n\nvoid scalar_gated_delta_attention(\n torch::Tensor q,\n torch::Tensor k,\n torch::Tensor v,\n torch::Tensor gate,\n torch::Tensor beta,\n torch::Tensor out) {\n\n int B = q.size(0);\n int T = q.size(1);\n int H = q.size(2);\n int D = q.size(3);\n\n dim3 grid(B, H, D);\n dim3 block(32);\n\n scalar_gated_delta_attention_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(\n q.data_ptr<float>(),\n k.data_ptr<float>(),\n v.data_ptr<float>(),\n gate.data_ptr<float>(),\n beta.data_ptr<float>(),\n out.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, T, H, D);\n}\n\"\"\"\n\n_cpp_ext = ext.load_inline(\n name=\"scalar_gated_delta_attention_v2\",\n cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCE,\n cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE,\n functions=[\"scalar_gated_delta_attention\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\", \"--use_fast_math\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self):\n super().__init__()\n\n def forward(self, q, k, v, gate, beta):\n q = q.contiguous()\n k = k.contiguous()\n v = v.contiguous()\n gate = gate.contiguous()\n beta = beta.contiguous()\n out = torch.empty_like(q)\n _cpp_ext.scalar_gated_delta_attention(q, k, v, gate, beta, out)\n return out\n" }
